Validating a Judge's Ruling Contrary to His Own Madhhab
Third scenario: judge's ruling contrary to his madhhab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-hakim rafi' li al-khilaf' to identify when a mufti can confirm a judge's ruling that contradicts his own madhhab.

Core Idea
A mujtahid judge's ruling, even if against his own madhhab, is binding and removes the dispute.

In this lesson, we examine a key scenario from Rasm al-Mufti: when a judge rules contrary to his own madhhab, the mufti must affirm the ruling's validity. This rests on the maxim that a judge's judgment resolves the dispute (rafi' li al-khilaf). Understanding this ruling helps a mufti navigate conflicts between personal ijtihad and judicial authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the judge was appointed without restriction to a madhhab and is a mujtahid.
The passage assumes these conditions are met.
2
Determine the subject of inquiry
The judge's ruling contradicts his own madhhab. Note that the mufti is asked about its validity.
The mufti's own madhhab may also differ.
3
Apply the maxim
Recall the consensus: 'Hukm al-hakim rafi' li al-khilaf'. This means the judgment brings finality.
The maxim applies only to matters of ijtihad.
4
Determine the mufti's response
The mufti must confirm the ruling's effectiveness (nafidh), even though it contradicts his own opinion.
The mufti does not adopt the ruling as his own fatwa; he acknowledges its legal effect.
5
Note the condition for validity
The matter must be open to ijtihad; if it contradicts a definitive text or consensus, the ruling is invalid.
This condition is an exception to the maxim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا قضى القاضي بغير مذهبه إذا ولى الإمام قاضياً ولم يقيده بمذهب بعينه، وكان القاضي مجتهداً، فقضى بما خالف مذهب غيره، نفذ قضاؤه مادامت المسألة مجتهداً فيها، فلو سئل المفتى أجاب بنفاذ قضاءه، ولو كان القضاء خلاف مذهبه. فهي الصورة الثَّالثةُ من الصُّور التى يُفتى فيها المفتى بغير مذهبه. وذلك لما اتفق عليه الفقهاء من أنّ حكم الحاكم أو قضاء القاضى رافع للخلاف. (۱) شرح عقود رسم المفتى ص ٩٤
When the judge rules contrary to his own madhhab: If the ruler appoints a judge and does not restrict him to a particular madhhab, and the judge is a mujtahid, and he rules contrary to the madhhab of another, his judgment is executed as long as the issue is one of ijtihad. So if the mufti is asked, he would answer that his judgment is effective, even if the judgment was contrary to his own madhhab. This is the third type of cases in which the mufti gives fatwa contrary to his own madhhab. This is because of the consensus of the jurists that the judgment of a ruler or the ruling of a judge resolves the dispute. (1) Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti, p. 94.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents the third scenario in which a mufti may issue a fatwa contrary to his own madhhab: when asked about the validity of a judge's ruling. The ruling must come from a judge appointed by the ruler without restriction to a specific madhhab, and the judge must be a mujtahid (or at least capable of ijtihad). If the judge rules on a matter that is open to ijtihad, his ruling is valid and enforceable (nafidh), even if it contradicts the judge's own usual madhhab. Consequently, a mufti, when asked about the validity of such a ruling, must affirm that it is effective, even though the mufti himself follows a different opinion. The basis for this is the well-known legal maxim, agreed upon by the jurists, that the judgment of a judge resolves the dispute (rafi' li al-khilaf). This principle means that once a judge has given a ruling on a matter of ijtihad, the ruling becomes binding and the difference of opinions is lifted with respect to that particular case. The mufti's confirmation does not require him to adopt the judge's ruling as his own fatwa; rather, he simply acknowledges the legal effect of the judicial decision.

Key Terms
قضى القاضي بغير مذهبه (qaḍā al-qāḍī bi-ghayr madhhabih): A judge issuing a ruling that contradicts the legal school he personally follows.
نفذ قضاؤه (nafidha qaḍā'uh): His judgment is executed/effective; the ruling is legally binding.
رافع للخلاف (rāfiʿ li al-khilāf): Removing the dispute; the judgment brings finality to the disagreement among scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a judge's ruling valid even if it contradicts his own madhhab?
A) When the ruler approves.
B) When the judge is a mujtahid and the matter is ijtihadi.
C) When the mufti agrees.
D) When the majority school holds the same view.

Key Takeaway
A mujtahid judge's ruling on a matter of ijtihad is binding and resolves the dispute, even if it contradicts his own madhhab.

When Can a Judge Overturn a Previous Ruling?
Classification of cases for overturning judicial rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a judicial ruling according to al-Kasani's categories and apply the rule that ijtihad-based rulings are not to be overturned.

Core Idea
A judge's ijtihad-based ruling is final; only a definitive text or consensus can overturn it.

This lesson presents a foundational rule from Hanafi jurisprudence on the finality of judicial rulings. When a second judge is asked to review a previous ruling, he must determine whether the original ruling was based on a definitive text (nass mufassar) or on ijtihad. The rule protects ijtihad-based rulings from being overturned by another ijtihad, preserving legal stability and preventing endless disputes.

Guided Path
1
Classify the matter of the original ruling
Determine if the ruling concerns a matter for which there is a definitive text (nass mufassar from Quran, mutawatir Sunnah, or ijma') or a matter open to ijtihad.
This is the first branching point in al-Kasani's analysis.
2
Check compliance with definitive texts
If the original ruling agrees with the definitive text or consensus, it is absolutely valid and must be enforced. If it contradicts them, it is absolutely invalid and must be overturned.
There is no room for further ijtihad here.
3
For ijtihad matters: verify consensus on ijtihad
If the matter is subject to ijtihad, check whether it is agreed (mujma' 'alayhi) that the matter is a proper field for ijtihad, or if there is dispute.
If there is dispute about whether ijtihad is allowed, the ruling may be overturned.
4
Apply the rule for agreed ijtihad matters
If the matter is unanimously considered a field of ijtihad, then the original ruling was valid by consensus (since all agree the judge can rule by his ijtihad). The second judge must enforce it and cannot overturn it based on his own differing ijtihad.
Overturning would require a definitive proof, which does not exist in ijtihad.
5
Understand the rationale
The rule prevents endless litigation and social disorder. If every new judge could overturn based on his own ijtihad, disputes would never end.
This is a necessary public interest (maslaha).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصول الإفتاء ۲۲۲ الافتاء بمذهب آخر فلو فتحنا باب نقض القضاء على أساس الآراء المختلفة، تبقى النزاع إلى ما لانهاية له، فكل قاض جديدٍ يمكن أن ينقض قضاء السابق على أساس رأيه. وبما أن المذاهب المختلفة لا يُقطع فى أحدها بالبطلان المحض، فإنّ الرأى المقضي به ترجّح على غيره بالقضاء الرافع للنزاع، فيبقى كما هو، إلا إذا كان مخالفاً للنصوص القطعية أو الإجماع، فلاسبيل إلى إقراره، لأنه يدخل حينئذ في الحكم بغير ما أنزل الله تعالى. ولكن في المسئلة تفصيل مبعثر فى كتب الفقه بجزئياته المختلفة، فلنذكرها بشيئ من التفصيل والله سبحانه هو الموفق للصواب. (1) قد فصل ملك العلماء الكاساني رحمه الله تعالى هذه المسئلة، فنحكى أولاً عبارته بتمامها، ثم نذكر إن شاء الله تعالى ما يتلخص منها بشيئ من الإيضاح والتفصيل . قال رحمه الله تعالى في "البدائع ": " وأمّا بيانُ ما ينفذ من القضايا وما يُنقض منها إذا رفع إلى قاض آخر، فنقول وبالله التوفيق: قضاء القاضي الأول لا يخلو إما أن وقع في فصل فيه نص مفسر من الكتاب العزيز والسنة المتواترة والإجماع، وإما أن وقع في فصل مجتهد فيه من ظواهر النصوص والقياس فإن وقع في فصل فيه نص مفسر العلامة الكاساني: هو أبو بكر بن مسعود بن أحمد، علاء الدين، ملك العلماء الكاساني، نسبة إلى بلدة كبيرة بتركستان خلف سيحون تفقه على العلامة محمد بن أحمد السمرقندي، صاحب "تحفة الفقهاء" الذى شرحه صاحب الترجمة باسم "بدائع الصنائع فصار من أجل مراجع الفقه الحنفي. وقيل إن صاحب "التحفة" زوج ابنته فاطمة العلامة الكاساني لما عرض شرح التحفة من عليه، وجعل المهر هذا الشرح. حتى اشتهر فيه أنه "شرح تحفته وتزوج ابنته" وأرسله السلطان نور الدين ابن زنكي إلى الحلاوية بحلب للإفادة. توفّي رحمه الله تعالى في عاشر رجب سنة ٥٨٧ هـ، ودفن بظاهر حلب عند قبر زوجته فاطمة. واشتهر أن الدّعاء عند قبرهما مستجاب. (ملخص) من الجواهر المضيئة ٤ : ٢٥ إلى ٢٨، والفوائد البهية ص٥٣) If من الكتاب أو الخبر المتواتر أو الإجماع، فإن وافق قضاؤه ذلك، نفذ ولا يحل له النقض، لأنه وقع صحيحاً قطعاً، وإن خالف شيئاً من ذلك يرده، لأنه وقع باطلاً قطعاً. وإن وقع في فصل مجتهد فيه، فلا يخلو إما أن كان مُجْمَعاً على كونه مجتهداً فيه، وإما أن كان مختلفاً فى كونه مجتهداً فيه. فإن كان ذلك مجمعاً على كونه محل الاجتهاد، فإما أن كان المجتهد فيه هو المقضي به، وإما أن كان نفس القضاء، فإن كان المجتهد فيه هو المقضي به فرفع قضاؤه إلى قاض آخر، لم يرده الثاني، بل ينفّذه لكونه قضاء مجمعاً على صحته، لما علم أنّ النّاس على اختلافهم في المسألة اتفقوا على أن للقاضي أن يقضي بأي الأقوال الذى مال إليه اجتهاده، فكان قضاءً مُجمَعاً على صحته. فلو نقضه إنما ينقضه بقوله ، وفى صحته اختلاف بين الناس، فلا يجوز نقض ما صح بالاتفاق بقول مختلف في صحته، ولأنه ليس مع الثانى دليل قطعي، بل اجتهادي، وصحة قضاء القاضي الأول ثبت بدليل قطعي، وهو إجماعهم على جواز القضاء بأي وجهِ اتّضح له، فلا يجوز نقض ما مضى بدليل قاطع بما فيه شبهة ولأنّ الضرورة توجب القول بلزوم القضاء المبني على الاجتهاد، وأن لا يجوز نقضه، لأنه لو جاز نقضه، يرفعه إلى قاض آخر يرى خلاف رأي الأول، فينقضه، ثمّ يرفعه المدعى إلى قاض آخر يرى خلاف رأي القاضي الثاني فينقض نقضه، ويقضى كما قضى الأول، فيؤدى إلى أن لا تندفع الخصومة والمنازعة أبداً، والمنازعة سبب الفساد، وما أدى إلى الفساد فساد. فإن كان رده القاضى الثانى، فرفعه إلى قاض ثالث، نفّذ قضاء القاضي الأول، وأبطل قضاء القاضي الثاني، لأن قضاء الأول صحيح، وقضاء الثاني بالرد باطل ....
Principles of Issuing Fatwas 222 Issuing Fatwas According to Another Madhhab. If we open the door to overturning judicial rulings on the basis of different opinions, disputes would continue endlessly, for every new judge could overturn the ruling of his predecessor based on his own opinion. Since different madhhabs do not hold any one of them to be absolutely invalid, the opinion that was ruled upon becomes preponderant by the ruling that ends the dispute, so it remains as it is, unless it contradicts definitive texts or consensus, for then there is no way to uphold it, because that would be ruling by other than what Allah revealed. However, the matter has details scattered in the books of fiqh in its various particulars, so let us mention them with some detail. Allah the Exalted is the Guide to correctness. (1) The king of scholars al-Kasani (may Allah have mercy on him) has detailed this issue, so let us first quote his full statement, then we will mention what may be gleaned from it with some clarification and detail, Allah willing. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said in al-Bada'i': "As for the explanation of which rulings are enforced and which are overturned when referred to another judge, we say – and success is from Allah –: The ruling of the first judge must either occur in a matter concerning which there is an explicit text from the Noble Book, the Mutawatir Sunnah, or consensus, or it must occur in a matter subject to ijtihad from the apparent meanings of texts and analogy. If it occurs in a matter that has an explicit text from the Book, Mutawatir Khabar, or consensus - The scholar al-Kasani: He is Abu Bakr ibn Mas'ud ibn Ahmad, 'Ala' al-Din, the king of scholars, al-Kasani, relative to a large town in Turkestan beyond the Syr Darya. He studied fiqh under the scholar Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Samarqandi, author of 'Tuhfat al-Fuqaha'' which the subject of this biography explained under the title 'Bada'i' al-Sana'i', and it became among the greatest references of Hanafi fiqh. It is said that the author of 'al-Tuhfa' married his daughter Fatima to the scholar al-Kasani when he presented the commentary of al-Tuhfa to him, and made this commentary the dower. It became famous that he 'explained his gift and married his daughter'. Sultan Nur al-Din ibn Zanki sent him to al-Halawiyya in Aleppo to teach. He died, may Allah have mercy on him, on the 10th of Rajab, 587 AH, and was buried outside Aleppo near the grave of his wife Fatima. It is well-known that supplication at their graves is answered. (Summary) from al-Jawahir al-Mudi'a 4:25-28 and al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyya p.53) If (his ruling) agrees with that, his ruling is enforced and it is not permissible for him to overturn it, because it occurred as absolutely correct. But if it contradicts any of that, he rejects it, because it occurred as absolutely invalid. And if it occurs in a matter subject to ijtihad, then it is either agreed upon that it is a matter of ijtihad, or disputed whether it is a matter of ijtihad. If it is agreed that it is a place of ijtihad, then either the subject of ijtihad is the matter ruled upon, or the ruling itself is the subject of ijtihad. If the subject of ijtihad is the matter ruled upon, and his ruling is brought to another judge, the second judge does not reject it; rather, he enforces it, because it is a ruling whose validity is agreed upon, since it is known that people, despite their differences in the issue, agree that the judge may rule according to any opinion that his ijtihad inclines to. So it is a ruling whose validity is agreed upon. If he were to overturn it, he would only overturn it by his own statement, and its validity is disputed among people. It is not permissible to overturn what has been established as valid by agreement with a statement whose validity is disputed. And because the second judge does not have a definitive proof, only an ijtihad-based one, while the validity of the first judge's ruling is established by a definitive proof – namely the consensus on the permissibility of ruling according to whichever aspect becomes clear to him. Thus, it is not permissible to overturn what has been decided by a definitive proof, with something that has doubt. And because necessity requires adopting the binding nature of a ruling based on ijtihad, and that it is not permissible to overturn it. For if overturning were permissible, it would be brought to another judge who holds the opposite view of the first, and he would overturn it, then the claimant would bring it to another judge holding the opposite view of the second, who would overturn the overturning and rule as the first did, leading to the dispute never being resolved. Dispute is a cause of corruption, and whatever leads to corruption is corrupt. If the second judge rejected it, and then it was brought to a third judge, he would enforce the ruling of the first judge and nullify the ruling of the second judge, because the first's ruling is valid and the second's ruling of rejection is invalid..."
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a methodological ruling (rasm) regarding the authority of a judge's decision when it is based on ijtihad. The author begins by highlighting the problem: if every new judge could overturn the rulings of his predecessor simply because he holds a different ijtihad, disputes would never end. The principle here is that the ruling of a judge, once given, is binding and cannot be overturned except if it contradicts a definitive text (nass mufassar) from the Qur'an, mutawatir hadith, or consensus (ijma'). In such a case, the ruling is absolutely invalid and must be rejected. If the ruling is in a matter open to ijtihad, and it is agreed that the matter is subject to ijtihad (i.e., mujma' 'alayhi), then the second judge cannot overturn it. The reason is that there is a consensus that a judge may rule according to any opinion his ijtihad leads him to. Thus, the first judge's ruling is valid by consensus, and the second judge's opinion is merely another ijtihad with no definitive proof. Overturning would lead to endless litigation, which is destructive. The passage also includes a biographical note about al-Kasani, the author of al-Bada'i', inserted by the author of the current work. The key takeaway: a judge's ijtihad-based ruling is final and must be enforced by subsequent judges to prevent endless disputes.

Key Terms
نص مفسر (nass mufassar): An explicit text from the Qur'an, mutawatir hadith, or consensus that clearly and unequivocally establishes a ruling, leaving no room for ijtihad.
مجتهد فيه (mujtahad fihi): A matter that is open to ijtihad, meaning there is no definitive textual ruling, and scholars may differ based on interpretation and analogy.
قضاء مجمع على صحته (qada' mujma' 'ala sihhatihi): A judicial ruling whose validity is agreed upon by all scholars because it was issued in a matter where the judge has the agreed-upon authority to rule according to his ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Kasani's classification, when can a second judge overturn the ruling of a first judge?
A) Whenever the second judge's ijtihad differs from the first.
B) Only if the first judge's ruling contradicts a definitive text or consensus.
C) Only if the first judge's ruling was in a matter not agreed to be subject to ijtihad.
D) The second judge can never overturn a first judge's ruling.

Key Takeaway
A judge's ijtihad-based ruling is binding and cannot be overturned by another judge's ijtihad; only contradiction with a definitive text or consensus permits overturning.

Finality of Qadi Judgments Based on Decisive Texts or Unanimous Ijtihad
Ruling on overturning qadi judgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on the finality of qadi judgments, distinguishing between absolutely valid judgments (based on text/mutawatir/ijma) and those in unanimously acknowledged ijtihad fields, and identify when a second qadi cannot overturn the first's ruling.

Core Idea
A qadi's judgment based on decisive evidence is absolute; ijtihad-based judgments are final if the field is unanimously considered ijtihad.

This passage from Usul al-Iftaa outlines a foundational principle for the finality of judicial decisions (qada') in the Hanafi school. It explains when a judgment is absolutely binding and cannot be overturned, and when it can be rejected. The key distinction is between cases decided by clear textual evidence or consensus, and those based on ijtihad in matters where all schools agree ijtihad is permissible. Understanding this rule is crucial for muftis and judges to maintain the integrity and finality of judicial rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of validity
The passage divides judgments into two categories: those that agree with the Quran, mutawatir hadith, or ijma (absolutely valid) and those that do not (absolutely invalid). For ijtihad-based judgments, it further qualifies based on whether the matter is unanimously considered a field of ijtihad.
Focus on the phrase 'فإن وافق قضاؤه ذلك نفذ' for the first category.
2
Analyze the condition for overturning ijtihad-based judgments
If the judgment is in a matter unanimously agreed to be subject to ijtihad, the second qadi cannot overturn it. The passage provides multiple reasons: the second qadi lacks decisive proof, the first's judgment is supported by consensus on ijtihad, and overturning leads to perpetual litigation.
Look for the reasoning markers: 'لأنه ليس مع الثانى دليل قطعي', 'ولأنّ الضرورة توجب'.
3
Examine the practical consequence (thamarah)
If overturning were allowed, cases could cycle through qadis indefinitely, never reaching finality. This would cause social corruption ('فساد'). The rule prevents this by making the first judgment binding.
This is a 'sadd al-dhara'i' type argument.
4
Derive the governing legal maxim
From this passage, we can extract a qawida: 'A judgment based on a decisive text or unanimous ijtihad cannot be overturned by another equally valid ijtihad.' This maxim governs the finality of qada'.
Formulate the maxim in Arabic and English.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الكتاب أو الخبر المتواتر أو الإجماع، فإن وافق قضاؤه ذلك، نفذ ولا يحل له النقض، لأنه وقع صحيحاً قطعاً، وإن خالف شيئاً من ذلك يرده، لأنه وقع باطلاً قطعاً. وإن وقع في فصل مجتهد فيه، فلا يخلو إما أن كان مُجْمَعاً على كونه مجتهداً فيه، وإما أن كان مختلفاً فى كونه مجتهداً فيه. فإن كان ذلك مجمعاً على كونه محل الاجتهاد، فإما أن كان المجتهد فيه هو المقضي به، وإما أن كان نفس القضاء، فإن كان المجتهد فيه هو المقضي به فرفع قضاؤه إلى قاض آخر، لم يرده الثاني، بل ينفّذه لكونه قضاء مجمعاً على صحته، لما علم أنّ النّاس على اختلافهم في المسألة اتفقوا على أن للقاضي أن يقضي بأي الأقوال الذى مال إليه اجتهاده، فكان قضاءً مُجمَعاً على صحته. فلو نقضه إنما ينقضه بقوله ، وفى صحته اختلاف بين الناس، فلا يجوز نقض ما صح بالاتفاق بقول مختلف في صحته، ولأنه ليس مع الثانى دليل قطعي، بل اجتهادي، وصحة قضاء القاضي الأول ثبت بدليل قطعي، وهو إجماعهم على جواز القضاء بأي وجهِ اتّضح له، فلا يجوز نقض ما مضى بدليل قاطع بما فيه شبهة ولأنّ الضرورة توجب القول بلزوم القضاء المبني على الاجتهاد، وأن لا يجوز نقضه، لأنه لو جاز نقضه، يرفعه إلى قاض آخر يرى خلاف رأي الأول، فينقضه، ثمّ يرفعه المدعى إلى قاض آخر يرى خلاف رأي القاضي الثاني فينقض نقضه، ويقضى كما قضى الأول، فيؤدى إلى أن لا تندفع الخصومة والمنازعة أبداً، والمنازعة سبب الفساد، وما أدى إلى الفساد فساد.
If it [the judgment] agrees with the Book, the mutawatir report, or consensus, it is executed and it is not permissible for him to overturn it, because it occurred as absolutely valid. But if it contradicts any of that, he rejects it because it occurred as absolutely invalid. If it occurs in a matter subject to ijtihad, it is either unanimously agreed to be subject to ijtihad, or there is disagreement on whether it is subject to ijtihad. If it is unanimously agreed to be a place of ijtihad, then either the matter subject to ijtihad is that which was judged, or the judgment itself. If the matter subject to ijtihad is that which was judged, and his judgment is brought to another qadi, the second does not reject it, but rather executes it because it is a judgment unanimously agreed to be valid, for it is known that people, despite their differences on the issue, agreed that the qadi may judge by whichever opinion his ijtihad inclines to, so it is a judgment unanimously agreed to be valid. If he were to overturn it, he would only overturn it by his own opinion, and its validity is disputed among people, so it is not permissible to overturn what is established as valid by agreement with an opinion whose validity is disputed. Also, because the second does not have a decisive proof, only an ijtihadi one, and the validity of the first qadi's judgment is established by a decisive proof, namely their consensus on the permissibility of judging by whichever aspect becomes clear to him, so it is not permissible to overturn what is established by a decisive proof with something that has doubt. Also, because necessity requires affirming the binding nature of a judgment based on ijtihad, and that it is not permissible to overturn it. If it were permissible to overturn it, the plaintiff would raise it to another qadi who holds the opposite opinion of the first, and he would overturn it, then the plaintiff would raise it to another qadi who holds the opposite opinion of the second, and he would overturn the overturning, and judge as the first judged, leading to the dispute and litigation never being resolved, and litigation is a cause of corruption, and whatever leads to corruption is corrupt.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the finality of qadi judgments. It first establishes that a judgment which aligns with the Quran, mutawatir hadith, or consensus is absolutely valid and cannot be overturned. If it contradicts these, it is absolutely invalid and must be rejected. Then it addresses ijtihad-based judgments. When the case is unanimously acknowledged as a field of ijtihad, and the ruling itself is what is being ijtihad upon, the second qadi cannot overturn the first qadi's decision. The reasoning is multi-fold: the first qadi's judgment is considered valid by consensus because all schools agree that a qadi may follow his ijtihad. To overturn it would be to replace an agreement with a disputed opinion. Moreover, the second qadi lacks decisive proof, while the first's judgment is supported by the decisive proof of consensus on the permissibility of ijtihad. Finally, overturning would lead to an endless cycle of litigation, causing societal corruption. This protects the finality of judgments in ijtihad matters.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): A judicial verdict or judgment issued by a qadi.
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Independent legal reasoning to derive a ruling from the sources.
نقض (naqd): Overturning or annulling a previous judicial decision.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a second qadi prohibited from overturning a first qadi's judgment in a matter unanimously agreed to be a field of ijtihad?
A) Because the first qadi's ijtihad is infallible.
B) Because the second qadi's ijtihad is automatically weaker.
C) Because the second qadi lacks a decisive proof and overturning would lead to endless litigation.
D) Because the first qadi's judgment is based on a clear text.

Key Takeaway
A qadi's judgment in a matter unanimously considered a field of ijtihad is final and cannot be overturned by another qadi, to preserve the finality of judicial decisions and prevent social disorder.

Overturning Judgments When the Ruling or Its Ijtihad Status Is Disputed
Rules for overturning judgments based on ijtihad status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between two scenarios for overturning a qadi's judgment based on the ijtihad status of the judgment itself and the underlying issue, and apply the Hanafi position regarding later consensus.

Core Idea
A second qadi may overturn a first's judgment if its permissibility was disputed, but not if the issue's ijtihad status is contested and he considers it settled.

This lesson examines a nuanced rule in Hanafi qada: when can a later judge overturn an earlier judgment? The source distinguishes situations where the judgment itself is a matter of ijtihad from those where the underlying issue's eligibility for ijtihad is debated. Using the classical example of selling a slave-mother (umm walad), we explore the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether later consensus (ijma' muta'akhkhir) can override earlier disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between (a) the judgment itself being ijtihad (e.g., interdiction over a free person) and (b) the issue's ijtihad status being contested (e.g., selling umm walad).
2
Analyze the first scenario
The first qadi's judgment was permitted only by some scholars, so it is subject to overturning by a second qadi with different ijtihad.
Unlike cases where the subject matter of the judgment was agreed upon.
3
Analyze the second scenario
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad on whether later consensus removes the issue from ijtihad.
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the second qadi may uphold or reject the first judgment.
4
Determine the ruling for the second qadi
If the second qadi considers the issue still ijtihad, he may enforce his own judgment; if he considers it settled, he rejects the first judgment.
This depends on his own usul view on later consensus.
5
Reflect on the usul principle
The effect of later consensus (ijma' muta'akhkhir) on earlier ikhtilaf is a key debate; the Hanafi school's relied-upon position is that it does not override.
This principle governs many fatwa scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان نفس القضاء مجتهداً فيه أنه يجوز أم لا؟ كما لو قضى بالحَجْرِ على الحُرّ، أو قضى على الغائب، إنّه يجوز للقاضى الثانى أن ينقض قضاء الأول إذا مال اجتهاده إلى خلاف اجتهاد الأول، لأن قضاءه هنا لم يجز بقول الكُل، بل بقولِ البعض دون البعض، فلم يكن جوازه متفقاً عليه، فكان محتملاً للنقض بمثله، بخلاف الفصل الأول، لأن جواز القضاء هناك ثبت بقول الكل، فكان متفقاً عليه، فلا يَحْتَمِلُ النّقض بقول البعض. ولأن المسألة إذا كانت مختلفاً فيها، فالقاضى بالقضاء يقطع أحد الاختلافين، ويجعله متفقاً عليه في الحكم بالقضاء المتفق على جوازه، وإذا كان نفس القضاء مختلفاً فيه، يرفع الخلاف بالخلاف. هذا إذا كان القضاء فى محل أجمعوا على كونه محل الاجتهاد. فأما إذا كان في محل اختلفوا أنه محل الاجتهاد أم لا؟ كبيع أم الولد، هل ينفذ قضاء القاضى أم لا؟ فعند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رحمهما الله ينفذ، لأنه محل الاجتهاد عندهما لاختلاف الصحابة في جواز بيعها، وعند محمد لا ينفذ لوقوع الاتفاق بعد ذلك من الصحابة وغيرهم على أنه لا يجوز بيعها، فخرج عن محل الاجتهاد. وهذا يرجع إلى أن الإجماع المتأخر هل يرفع الخلاف المتقدم؟ عندهما لا يرفع وعنده يرفع فكان هذا الفصل مختلفاً في كونه مجتهداً فيه، فيُنظر إن كان من رأي القاضى الثانى أنه يجتهد فيه، ينفذ قضاؤه، ولا يرده لما ذكرنا في سائر المجتهدات المتفق عليها، وإن كان من رأيه أنه خرج عن حد الاجتهاد، وصار متفقاً عليه لا ينفذ، بل يرده لأن عنده أن قضاءه ليس بقضاء معتبر.
And if the judgment itself is subject to ijtihad—whether it is permissible or not? For example, if a qadi passed a judgment of interdiction over a free person, or passed a judgment against an absent person—it is permissible for the second qadi to overturn the first qadi's judgment if his ijtihad inclines to the contrary of the first's ijtihad, because here the permissibility of his judgment was not established by the consensus of all, but rather by the opinion of some and not others, so its permissibility was not agreed upon, making it subject to being overturned by a similar [contrary ijtihad], unlike the first case, because there the permissibility of the judgment was established by consensus, so it was agreed upon, and it does not bear being overturned by the opinion of some. And because when the issue is subject to disagreement, the qadi who judges cuts off one of the two disagreements and makes it agreed upon regarding the ruling with a judgment agreed to be permissible, but when the judgment itself is subject to disagreement, disagreement is removed by [another] disagreement. This is when the judgment is in a place unanimously agreed to be subject to ijtihad. But if it is in a place where they disagree whether it is subject to ijtihad or not—like the sale of umm walad: does the qadi's judgment take effect or not? According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on them), it takes effect, because it is a place of ijtihad in their view due to the disagreement of the Companions on the permissibility of selling her. According to Muhammad, it does not take effect because later consensus occurred from the Companions and others that it is not permissible to sell her, so it exited from being a place of ijtihad. This returns to the issue: does later consensus remove earlier disagreement? According to them [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf], it does not remove; according to him [Muhammad], it does. So this chapter is disputed as to whether it is subject to ijtihad. It is then considered: if the second qadi holds the view that it is subject to ijtihad, he executes his judgment and does not reject it, for the reasons we mentioned for other unanimously agreed ijtihad matters. But if he holds the view that it has exited the bounds of ijtihad and become agreed upon, then he does not execute it; rather, he rejects it, because in his view his [the first qadi's] judgment is not a valid judgment.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage continues the earlier discussion, now addressing two further scenarios. First, when the judgment itself (e.g., the qadi's jurisdiction over a free person or an absent party) is a matter of ijtihad—meaning scholars disagree on whether such an action constitutes a valid judgment. In this case, the second qadi may overturn the first's decision if his own ijtihad differs, because the first's judgment was not universally accepted as permissible. This contrasts with the earlier case where the subject matter of the judgment was ijtihad and all agreed the qadi could act. Second, when there is disagreement on whether the issue itself is open to ijtihad, using the example of selling a slave mother (umm walad). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider it still a matter of ijtihad because the Companions differed. Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, asserts that a later consensus formed prohibiting the sale, removing it from ijtihad. The ruling for the second qadi depends on his own view: if he considers the issue ijtihad, he upholds the first judgment; if he considers it settled, he rejects it. This highlights a key Hanafi usul dispute about whether later consensus overrides earlier disagreement.

Key Terms
أم الولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master.
إجماع متأخر (ijma' muta'akhkhir): A consensus formed after an earlier period of disagreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, why does the sale of umm walad remain a valid ijtihad issue for a qadi?
A) Because the Prophet (saw) explicitly permitted it
B) Because later consensus affirmed the permissibility
C) Because the Companions themselves had differing opinions on its permissibility
D) Because it is explicitly mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
A second qadi may overturn a first judgment only when the judgment itself was disputed; if the issue's ijtihad status is contested, the second qadi's own view on later consensus determines whether he upholds or rejects.

advanced
10 min
Validity of Judicial Rulings in Ijtihadi Matters and Later Consensus
Critical refinement of ruling validity and later consensus effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply al-Kasani's four-point classification to determine the validity of a judicial ruling and evaluate the effect of later consensus on prior ijtihadi disagreement.

Core Idea
A judicial ruling in a valid ijtihadi matter stands even if later consensus opposes it.

This lesson delves into a crucial principle of Hanafi judicial and fatwa methodology: when does a judge's ruling remain effective despite disagreement? The passage from 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti presents al-Kasani's classification of four scenarios and examines whether a later consensus can override earlier ijtihad. Understanding this helps in determining the binding force of past judgments and the scope of valid ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios of ruling validity
Al-Kasani classifies cases based on whether the matter is settled by consensus, unanimously recognized as ijtihadi, disputed in its ijtihadi status, or where the ruling itself is ijtihadi. For each, the ruling is either effective or void.
Focus on the third scenario—disagreement on ijtihadi status—and the fourth—ruling itself ijtihadi.
2
Analyze the third scenario: disagreement on ijtihadi status
When there is disagreement on whether the matter is ijtihadi, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the ruling effective; Muhammad holds it ineffective.
This is the core intra-Hanafi dispute addressed in the passage.
3
Understand the concept of later consensus (ijmā' lāḥiq)
Later consensus occurs when a later generation of scholars agrees on one of the earlier disputed positions. The passage uses the example of selling Umm al-Walad.
The key question: does later consensus retroactively nullify the earlier ijtihad status?
4
Compare the positions on later consensus
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say later consensus does not remove prior disagreement; the matter remains ijtihadi. Muhammad appears to consider later consensus as rendering the first ruling ineffective unless endorsed.
Al-Sarakhsi's reasoning: the consensus of the Followers lacks the strength to overrule the Companions' disagreement.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position
The author confirms that the position of the two Shaykhs is preponderant (rājiḥ): the first ruling stands without needing a second judge's endorsement.
Muhammad's view requires endorsement, but the author clarifies it does not undermine the direct effectiveness under the two Shaykhs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصول الإفتاء
۲۲۰
الافتاء بمذهب آخر
الأول وقع مخالفاً للإجماع، فكان باطلاً.
ومن مشايخنا من فصل فى المجتهدات تفصيلاً آخر، فقال: إن كان الاجتهاد شنيعاً مستنكراً، جاز للقاضى الثانى أن ينقض قضاء الأول. وهذا فيه نظر، لأنه إذا صح كونه محل الاجتهاد، فلا معنى للفصل بين مجتهد ومجتهد، لأن ما ذكرنا من المعنى لا يوجب الفصل بينهما، فينبغي أن لا يجوز للثاني نقض قضاء الأول، لأن قضاءه صادف محل الاجتهاد. (۱)
.1
وحاصل ماذكره الكاساني رحمه الله تعالى ما يأتي:
إن كان القضاء فى مسئلةٍ مُجْمَعِ عليها، فما وافق الإجماع نفذ، وما خالفه بطل.
إن كان المقضى به مجتهداً فيه، ولا خلاف في كونه مجتهداً فيه، نفذ القضاء بالاتفاق.
.{
إن كان هناك خلاف فى كون المقضى به مجتهداً فيه، نفذ القضاء عند أبي حنيفة وأبى يوسف، ولم ينفذ عند محمد رحمهم الله تعالى. إن كان القضاء نفسه مجتهداً فيه، مثل القضاء على الغائب، والحجر على الحر، لم ينفذ القضاء عند من لا يجوزه وإن الأولى والثانية من هذه النقاط الأربع لا يحتاجان إلى شرح وتفصيل، كما هو ظاهر.
هل يرتفع الخلاف المتقدم بالإجماع اللاحق؟ أمّا النّقطة الثّالثة، فهى متعلقة بما إذا كان هناك خلاف في المسئلة
(۱) بدائع الصنائع كتاب أدب القاضي، فصل: ما ينفذ من القضايا وما لا ينفذ ٥ : ٤٥٧ ، ٤٥٨

الافتاء بمذهب آخر
أصول الإفتاء في عهد الصحابة والتابعين، ثم وقع الإجماع على أحد المذهبين. مثل بيع أمّ الولد، كان فيه خلاف فى عهد الصحابة هل يحوز أم لا؟ فكان عمر رضى الله تعالى عنه يقول: إنّ بيعها لا يجوز، وكان على رضى الله تعالى عنه يجوز بيعها. ثم وقع الإجماع في عهد التابعين على عدم جوازه فيقول الإمام أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف رحمهما الله تعالى: إن الإجماع اللاحق لا يرفع ال المتقدم، فتبقى المسألة مجتهداً فيها بالرغم من الإجماع الذى وقع أخيراً، وعلّله السَّرَحْسِيُّ رحمه الله تعالى بأنه ليس لإجماع التابعين من القوة ما يرفع الخلاف الذى كان بين الصحابة رضوان الله عليهم. فلو قضى القاضي بجواز بيع أم الولد نفذ عند الشيخين، لكونه قضاء فى فصل مجتهد فيه.

(1)
الخلاف
(1) المبسوط للسرخسي، باب البيوع الفاسدة ١٣:٥ وليتنبه أن بعض الفقهاء أفتوا بقول محمد رحمه الله تعالى في القضاء ببيع أم الولد أنه لا ينفذ. وقال بعضهم إنه يتوقف على إمضاء قاض آخر، لأنه وقع الاختلاف في كون المسئلة اجتهادية كان القضاء مختلفا فيه فإن أمضاه القاضي الآخر كان هذا القضاء الثانى فى مجتهد فيه، فينفذ هذا القضاء الثانى وراجع لتفصيله ردالمحتار، باب الاستيلاد، مطلب فى قضاء القاضى بغير مذهبه فقره ۱۷۰۱۲ وكتاب القضاء، فصل في الحبس، فقره ٢٦٣٠٠ ولكن لوسلّم هذا على مذهب الشيخين دخل القسم الثالث من الأقسام الأربعة المذكورة دائماً في القسم الرابع الذى يتوقف فيه القضاء على إمضاء قاض آخر، كما سيأتي بيانه في الفقرة الآتية، وهذا خلاف المفروض. فلعل هذا القول مبني على قول محمد أيضاً، فإنه لا يقول بنفاذ القضاء الأوّل لكونه مخالفاً للإجماع عنده، ولكن لما كان عدم نفاذه مجتهداً فيه لاختلاف الشيخين، وقضى القاضى الثانى بنفاذه على مذهبهما، صار هذا القضاء الثاني في مسئلة مجتهد فيها، فحينئذ ينفذ قضاؤه على مذهب محمد رحمه الله تعالى. أما على مذهب الشيخين، وهو الراجح كما سيأتى إن شاء الله تعالى، فلايتوقف نفاذ القضاء الأوّل على إمضاء القاضي الثاني، ولهذا ذكر الكاساني رحمه الله تعالى نفاذه بدون أن يقول بتوقفه على قاض آخر. والحاصل أنّ القضاء في مثل هذا ينفذ عند الشيخين مباشرةً، ويتوقف عند محمّد على إمضاء قاض آخر، كما يتوقف عليه في القسم الرابع الآتى ذكره في النقطة الرابعة، فليتأمل، والله سبحانه أعلم.
Principles of Issuing Fatwa 220 - Issuing Fatwa According to Another Madhhab. The first [ruling] occurred contrary to consensus, so it was invalid. Among our scholars, some made a different detailed distinction in matters of ijtihad, saying: If the ijtihad is egregiously objectionable, then the second judge may overturn the ruling of the first. But this is questionable, because if the matter is truly a locus of ijtihad, there is no meaning to distinguishing between one ijtihad and another, because the meaning we mentioned does not necessitate distinction between them. So it should not be permissible for the second to overturn the ruling of the first, because his ruling struck a locus of ijtihad. (1) .1 The summary of what al-Kasani (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned is as follows: If the ruling concerns a matter of consensus, then what agrees with consensus is effective, and what contradicts it is void. If the subject matter is a matter of ijtihad, and there is no disagreement that it is a matter of ijtihad, the ruling is effective by agreement. { If there is disagreement about whether the subject matter is a matter of ijtihad, the ruling is effective according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but not according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them all). If the ruling itself is a matter of ijtihad, such as a judgment against an absent person or placing a free person under guardianship, the ruling is not effective according to those who do not permit it. The first and second of these four points do not need explanation and detail, as is apparent. Does the prior disagreement cease due to later consensus? As for the third point, it relates to when there is disagreement about the matter... (1) Bada'i' al-Sana'i', Kitab Adab al-Qadi, Chapter: What is Effective and What is Not from Judgments, 5:457, 458. Issuing Fatwa According to Another Madhhab - Principles of Issuing Fatwa in the Era of the Companions and Followers, then consensus occurred on one of the two schools. For example, the sale of Umm al-Walad: there was disagreement in the era of the Companions whether it is permissible or not. 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) said that its sale is not permissible, while 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) allowed its sale. Then consensus occurred in the era of the Followers on its impermissibility. Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on them) say: Later consensus does not remove the prior disagreement, so the matter remains a matter of ijtihad despite the consensus that occurred later. Al-Sarakhsi (may Allah have mercy on him) explained this by saying that the consensus of the Followers does not have the strength to remove the disagreement that existed among the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them all). So if a judge ruled on the permissibility of selling Umm al-Walad, it is effective according to the two Shaykhs, because it is a ruling in a matter of ijtihad. Note that some jurists issued fatwa according to the opinion of Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) that a judgment regarding the sale of Umm al-Walad is not effective. And some said that it depends on the endorsement of another judge, because there is disagreement about whether the matter is a matter of ijtihad? The ruling itself is disputed, so if another judge endorses it, then this second ruling occurs in a matter of ijtihad, and thus this second ruling is effective. For details, refer to Radd al-Muhtar, Bab al-Istilad, Matalib fi Qada' al-Qadi bi Ghayr Madhhabihi, paragraph 17012, and Kitab al-Qada', Fasl fi al-Habs, paragraph 26300. But if this is accepted according to the school of the two Shaykhs, then the third section of the four mentioned sections would always enter into the fourth section, where the ruling depends on the endorsement of another judge, as will be explained in the following paragraph, and this is contrary to the assumption. So perhaps this opinion is also based on the opinion of Muhammad, because he does not say that the first ruling is effective since it is, in his view, contrary to consensus. But since its ineffectiveness is a matter of ijtihad due to the disagreement of the two Shaykhs, and the second judge ruled for its effectiveness according to their school, this second ruling becomes a matter of ijtihad, and then his ruling is effective according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him). As for the school of the two Shaykhs, which is the preponderant as will come, Allah willing, the effectiveness of the first ruling does not depend on the endorsement of a second judge. For this reason, al-Kasani (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned its effectiveness without saying that it depends on another judge. The conclusion is that a ruling in such a matter is directly effective according to the two Shaykhs, while according to Muhammad it depends on the endorsement of another judge, just as it depends in the fourth section mentioned in the fourth point. So ponder this. And Allah Most High knows best.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage from 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti discusses the validity of judicial rulings in matters where there is disagreement (ijtihad) and the impact of a later consensus on earlier disagreement. The author, Mufti Muhammad Taqi Usmani, begins by critiquing a view that allows a second judge to overturn a first judge's ruling if the ijtihad is 'egregiously objectionable'. He argues that once a matter is established as a valid locus of ijtihad, there is no basis for such distinction; the ruling should stand. He then summarizes al-Kasani's four-point classification: (1) If the matter is settled by consensus and the ruling agrees, it is effective; if it contradicts, it is void. (2) If the matter is unanimously recognized as ijtihadi, the ruling is effective by agreement. (3) If there is disagreement about whether the matter is ijtihadi, the ruling is effective according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf but not according to Muhammad. (4) If the ruling itself is ijtihadi (e.g., judgment against an absent person), it is not effective according to those who disallow it. The author then examines whether a later consensus (ijma') can remove the earlier disagreement, using the example of selling Umm al-Walad. He explains that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that later consensus does not retroactively nullify the earlier ijtihad status; therefore, a judge's ruling allowing the sale remains effective according to them. Al-Sarakhsi's reason is that the consensus of the Followers lacks the force to override the Companions' disagreement. The passage also discusses Muhammad's position that such rulings are ineffective or require endorsement by another judge. The author clarifies that the relied-upon (raajih) position is that of the two Shaykhs: the first ruling is directly effective without needing a second judge's endorsement. This is a methodological rule (rasm al-mufti) for determining the binding nature of judgments in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
قضاء (qaḍā'): A judicial ruling or judgment issued by a qāḍī.
اجتهاد (ijtihād): Legal reasoning by a qualified scholar to derive a ruling when no clear text exists.
إجماع لاحق (ijmā' lāḥiq): A consensus reached by scholars of a later generation on a matter previously disputed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two Shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), what is the ruling on a judge's decision in a matter where there is disagreement about whether it is a matter of ijtihad?
A) The decision is void and must be overturned.
B) The decision is effective and binding.
C) The decision is conditional on a second judge's endorsement.
D) The decision is only valid if it agrees with later consensus.

Key Takeaway
Valid judicial rulings made in ijtihadi matters remain effective even if later consensus contradicts them; later consensus does not retroactively nullify the ijtihad.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
advanced
6 min
Later Consensus and Earlier Ijtihad: The Hanafi Rule
Discussion of later consensus and earlier ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that later consensus does not nullify earlier valid ijtihad, using the criterion of ambiguity of evidence (ishribah al-dalil) to determine whether a matter remains subject to ijtihad.

Core Idea
Later consensus does not erase earlier valid ijtihad; only ambiguity of evidence matters.

The principle that later consensus (ijma' lahik) can override earlier disagreement seems straightforward, but the Hanafi school has a nuanced position: the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold that it does not. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute using the classical example of selling umm al-walad and the key criterion for determining what counts as a legitimate field of ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two positions
First, state the core issue: does a consensus that forms after a period of disagreement among earlier scholars (here, the Companions) remove that earlier ikhtilaf? Then identify the two positions: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no; Muhammad al-Shaybani says yes.
The practical consequence is whether a judge's ruling that follows the earlier dissenting view is enforceable.
2
Examine the example of bay' umm al-walad
The example: 'Umar held it impermissible, 'Ali held it permissible. Later, the Followers (tabi'un) reached consensus on impermissibility. Understand how the dispute played out in this concrete case.
This example illustrates the tension between early ikhtilaf and later ijma'.
3
Analyze the reasoning of the two shaykhs
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that the later consensus is not strong enough to overturn the Companions' disagreement. The criterion for ijtihad is not the fact of disagreement but the ambiguity of the evidence (ishribah al-dalil). Since the Companions' views were based on valid evidence, the matter remains a field of ijtihad.
Al-Sarakhsi explained: *laysa li ijma' al-tabi'ina min al-quwwa ma yarfa' al-khilaf*.
4
Understand Muhammad al-Shaybani's objection
Imam Muhammad holds that later consensus is final and binding, so it removes earlier disagreement. Therefore, a ruling contrary to that consensus is invalid and unenforceable.
This leads to differing practical outcomes for the judge's ruling.
5
Apply the tarjih and identify the mufta bihi
The author notes that the view of the two shaykhs is rajih (preponderant) because it aligns with the usul principle that the criterion for ijtihad is ambiguity of evidence. Many later Hanafi jurists, including Imam Muhammad himself in some statements, support this criterion.
See the reference from al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya and al-Sarakhsi.

الافتاء بمذهب آخر
أصول الإفتاء في عهد الصحابة والتابعين، ثم وقع الإجماع على أحد المذهبين. مثل بيع أمّ الولد، كان فيه خلاف فى عهد الصحابة هل يحوز أم لا؟ فكان عمر رضى الله تعالى عنه يقول: إنّ بيعها لا يجوز، وكان على رضى الله تعالى عنه يجوز بيعها. ثم وقع الإجماع في عهد التابعين على عدم جوازه فيقول الإمام أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف رحمهما الله تعالى: إن الإجماع اللاحق لا يرفع ال المتقدم، فتبقى المسألة مجتهداً فيها بالرغم من الإجماع الذى وقع أخيراً، وعلّله السَّرَحْسِيُّ رحمه الله تعالى بأنه ليس لإجماع التابعين من القوة ما يرفع الخلاف الذى كان بين الصحابة رضوان الله عليهم. فلو قضى القاضي بجواز بيع أم الولد نفذ عند الشيخين، لكونه قضاء فى فصل مجتهد فيه.

أصول الإفتاء

الافتاء بمذهب آخر
أمّا الإمام محمد رحمه الله تعالى، فيقول: إن الإجماع اللاحق يرفع الخلاف المتقدم، فلا تبقى المسألة مجتهداً فيها بعد وقوع الإجماع على أحد المذهبين. ولهذا لو قضى القاضى بجواز بيع أم الولد، لم ينفذ لكونه خلاف الإجماع. وبما أن كثيراً من الفقهاء الحنفية أفتوا بنفاذ القضاء بقول شريح في قبول شهادة النساء في الحدود والقصاص، كما سيأتي، بعد أن وقع الإجماع على عدم قبولها، فالظاهر أنه يستلزم أن يكون الفتوى على قول الشيخين رحمهما الله تعالى. وقول الشيخين أرجح دليلاً، لما ذكر غير واحدٍ من الفقهاء، ومنهم الإمام الله محمد رحمه تعالى من أن العبرة في كون المحل مجتهداً فيه اشتباه الدليل، لاحقيقة الخلاف. جاء في الفتاوى الهنديّة: "وفي المنتقى يُشير إلى أنّ العبرة لاشتباه الدليل لالحقيقة الاختلاف، وهكذا ذكر محمد رحمه الله تعالى في الجامع وفى السير الكبير. وهكذا ذكره صاحب الأقضية. صورة ما ذكر في السير: لو رأى إمام من أئمة المسلمين أن يقبل الجزية من مشركي العرب وقبل، جاز، وإن كان هذا خطأ عند الكل، لأنه موضع الاجتهاد. كذا في الذخيرة. " ولانستطيع أن نقول إنّ ماذهب إليه بعض الصحابة أو التابعين بخلاف ما وقع عليه الإجماع لاحقاً، لم يكن مبنياً على أي دليل، أو لم يكن موضع الاشتباه، فإن القول بلا دليل أو في غير موضع الاشتباه ضلال لا يتصور من خير القرون، والله سبحانه أعلم.
(۱) الفتاوى الهندية ٣:٣٥٧
Issuing fatwa according to another madhhab 
The principles of ifta in the era of the Companions and Followers, then consensus occurred on one of the two madhhabs. For example, the sale of umm al-walad: there was disagreement in the era of the Companions whether it is permissible or not. Umar ibn al-Khattab said it is not permissible, and Ali ibn Abi Talib said it is permissible. Then consensus occurred in the era of the Followers on its non-permissibility. So Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say: later consensus does not remove the earlier disagreement, so the matter remains subject to ijtihad despite the consensus that occurred later. Al-Sarakhsi explained that the consensus of the Followers does not have the strength to remove the disagreement that was among the Companions. So if a judge ruled to permit the sale of umm al-walad, it is enforceable according to the two shaykhs, because it is a ruling in a matter subject to ijtihad. 
(1) Al-Mabsut by al-Sarakhsi, chapter on invalid sales 13:5. It should be noted that some jurists gave fatwa according to the view of Muhammad that a ruling permitting sale of umm al-walad is not enforceable. Some said it depends on endorsement by another judge, because there is disagreement whether the matter is subject to ijtihad; if another judge endorses it, this second ruling is in a matter subject to ijtihad, so it is enforceable. For details, see Radd al-Muhtar, chapter on istilad, section on ruling by a judge against his madhhab, paragraph 17012, and Kitab al-Qada, chapter on imprisonment, paragraph 26300. But if this is conceded according to the two shaykhs, the third division of the four divisions always falls into the fourth division, which depends on endorsement by another judge, as will be explained in the next paragraph, and this contradicts the assumption. Perhaps this view is based on the opinion of Muhammad as well, because he does not consider the first ruling enforceable due to its opposition to consensus. But since its non-enforceability is a matter of ijtihad due to the disagreement of the two shaykhs, and a second judge rules to enforce it according to their madhhab, this second ruling is a matter subject to ijtihad, so his ruling is enforceable according to Muhammad. As for the two shaykhs' opinion, which is the preponderant as will be shown, the enforcement of the first ruling does not depend on endorsement by a second judge. Therefore al-Kasani mentioned its enforceability without conditioning it on another judge. The conclusion is that a ruling in such a case is directly enforceable according to the two shaykhs, but according to Muhammad, it depends on endorsement by another judge, as in the fourth division to be mentioned. So reflect, and Allah knows best. 
Principles of Ifta 227 
Issuing fatwa according to another madhhab 
As for Imam Muhammad, he says that later consensus removes the earlier disagreement, so the matter no longer remains subject to ijtihad after consensus on one of the two opinions. Therefore if a judge rules to permit the sale of umm al-walad, it is not enforceable because it contradicts consensus. And since many Hanafi jurists gave fatwa to enforce the ruling according to Shuraih's opinion regarding acceptance of women's testimony in hudud and qisas, as will come, after consensus occurred on its non-acceptance, it appears that fatwa must be according to the two shaykhs’ opinion. The two shaykhs' opinion is stronger in evidence, as mentioned by many jurists, including Imam Muhammad, that the criterion for a matter being subject to ijtihad is the ambiguity of evidence, not the reality of disagreement. It is stated in al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya: "In al-Muntaqa it is indicated that the criterion is the ambiguity of evidence, not the existence of disagreement. Similarly Muhammad mentioned in al-Jami' and al-Siyar al-Kabir, and so did the author of al-Aqdiyya. The example mentioned in al-Siyar: if an imam of the Muslims thought it permissible to accept jizya from Arab polytheists and accepted it, it is permissible, even if it is considered wrong by all, because it is a place of ijtihad. Thus in al-Dhakhira." And we cannot say that what some Companions or Followers held contrary to later consensus was not based on any evidence or was not a place of ambiguity, because a statement without evidence or in a place without ambiguity is misguidance, inconceivable from the best of generations. Allah knows best. 
(1) al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya 3:357
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage discusses a rasm al-mufti rule: how should a jurist deal with an issue that was subject to disagreement among the Companions but later a consensus (ijma') emerged on one opinion? The specific example is the sale of umm al-walad (a slave woman who has borne a child to her master). Among the Companions, 'Umar ibn al-Khattab held it impermissible, while 'Ali ibn Abi Talib held it permissible. Later, consensus occurred among the Followers (tabi'in) that such sale is impermissible. 

The author then presents the positions of the early Hanafi authorities. Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf (the 'two shaykhs') hold that a later consensus does not override the earlier disagreement. The reason, as explained by al-Sarakhsi, is that the consensus of a later generation lacks the strength to nullify the disagreement of the Companions. Therefore, the issue remains a matter of ijtihad (mujtahad fihi), and a judge's ruling permitting the sale would be valid and enforceable. 

Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, on the other hand, holds that later consensus does remove earlier disagreement, so the issue is no longer subject to ijtihad. Consequently, any ruling that contradicts the consensus would be invalid. 

The author then addresses a nuance: some later Hanafi jurists said that a ruling from the first judge might require endorsement by a second judge to be enforceable. However, according to the two shaykhs, the first ruling is directly enforceable without endorsement. The difference arises from whether the issue is still considered muijtahad fihi. 

The author supports the view of the two shaykhs, citing both Imam Muhammad's own statement that the criterion for ijtihad is the ambiguity of the evidence (ishribah al-dalil) rather than the mere fact of disagreement, and references from the Fatawa Hindiyya and other works. The conclusion is that later consensus does not erase the validity of earlier ijtihad, as the earlier views were based on valid evidence. This is the relied-upon (rajih) position in the Hanafi school for the purpose of issuing fatwas and judicial rulings.

Key Terms
بيع أم الولد (bay' umm al-walad): Sale of a slave woman who has borne a child to her master; a specific legal example in the text where there was earlier ikhtilaf among Companions followed by later consensus.
مجتهد فيه (mujtahad fihi): A matter where legitimate ijtihad is permitted due to the presence of ambiguous evidence (ishribah al-dalil), not simply because there is disagreement.
الإجماع اللاحق (al-ijma' al-lahiq): A consensus that emerges after a period of disagreement among earlier scholars, e.g., the consensus of the Followers after disagreement among the Companions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), if a later consensus emerges on a matter previously disputed among the Companions, what is the status of a judge's ruling that follows the earlier dissenting view?
A) It is invalid because consensus is binding and overrides earlier disagreement.
B) It is valid and enforceable without further endorsement.
C) It requires confirmation by a second judge to be enforceable.
D) It is null and void because it contradicts the later consensus.

Key Takeaway
Later consensus does not erase earlier valid ijtihad; the criterion for ijtihad is ambiguity of evidence, and rulings based on earlier ikhtilaf remain enforceable according to the preponderant Hanafi view.

Effect of Later Ijma on the Ijtihadi Status of a Mas'ala
Discusses the effect of later consensus on earlier ikhtilaf and the criterion for ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida that the criterion for ijtihad is ambiguity of evidence, and evaluate the effect of later ijma on earlier ikhtilaf according to Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
Later consensus removes earlier ikhtilaf, but the true criterion for ijtihad is ambiguity in the evidence, not just disagreement.

This passage from Imam Muhammad Taqi Usmani's Usul al-Ifta tackles a key debate in Hanafi rasm al-mufti: when does a matter cease to be open to ijtihad? Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani argues that later ijma on one of two opinions eliminates the earlier disagreement, making a contrary verdict unenforceable. Yet, many Hanafi jurists later acted on a weaker opinion after consensus, prompting a discussion on whether ambiguity of evidence (ishtibah al-dalil) is the true benchmark. By working through this lesson, you will learn to navigate the tension between later consensus and the enduring validity of earlier ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida
Begin by reading the Arabic of the qawida: 'العبرة في كون المحل مجتهداً فيه اشتباه الدليل، لا حقيقة الخلاف'. Understand that the criterion for a matter being ijtihadi is ambiguity in the evidence, not the mere fact of disagreement.
The qawida is from Imam Muhammad as cited in al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya.
2
Identify the exception (istithna)
Note that Imam Muhammad also states: if later consensus (ijma') occurs on one of the two opinions, the matter ceases to be ijtihadi. Thus, the qawida has an exception: established consensus overrides ambiguity.
This is the core intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf in the passage.
3
Apply the qawida to the examples
Work through the two examples: (a) sale of umm al-walad – after consensus on prohibition, a judge's contrary verdict is void; (b) women's testimony in hudud and qisas – the two shaykhs' view (rejecting it) is preferred because later consensus supports it.
Even Shuraih's earlier ijtihad is not grounded in clear evidence, but later consensus removes ambiguity.
4
Analyze the defense of earlier scholars
The author insists that earlier Companions and Followers whose views were later overridden by ijma were not acting without evidence. Their ijtihad was based on ambiguous evidence at the time.
This shows that the qawida (ambiguity criterion) protects the legitimacy of earlier ijtihad even if later consensus emerges.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Conclude that for fatwa, when later consensus is clearly established, the consensus opinion must be followed. However, the ambiguity criterion remains the foundational test for whether a matter was ever subject to ijtihad.
The passage indicates that 'the statement of the two shaykhs' (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is stronger, but the effect of later ijma is accepted for enforcement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمّا الإمام محمد رحمه الله تعالى، فيقول: إن الإجماع اللاحق يرفع الخلاف المتقدم، فلا تبقى المسألة مجتهداً فيها بعد وقوع الإجماع على أحد المذهبين. ولهذا لو قضى القاضى بجواز بيع أم الولد، لم ينفذ لكونه خلاف الإجماع. وبما أن كثيراً من الفقهاء الحنفية أفتوا بنفاذ القضاء بقول شريح في قبول شهادة النساء في الحدود والقصاص، كما سيأتي، بعد أن وقع الإجماع على عدم قبولها، فالظاهر أنه يستلزم أن يكون الفتوى على قول الشيخين رحمهما الله تعالى. وقول الشيخين أرجح دليلاً، لما ذكر غير واحدٍ من الفقهاء، ومنهم الإمام الله محمد رحمه تعالى من أن العبرة في كون المحل مجتهداً فيه اشتباه الدليل، لاحقيقة الخلاف. جاء في الفتاوى الهنديّة: "وفي المنتقى يُشير إلى أنّ العبرة لاشتباه الدليل لالحقيقة الاختلاف، وهكذا ذكر محمد رحمه الله تعالى في الجامع وفى السير الكبير. وهكذا ذكره صاحب الأقضية. صورة ما ذكر في السير: لو رأى إمام من أئمة المسلمين أن يقبل الجزية من مشركي العرب وقبل، جاز، وإن كان هذا خطأ عند الكل، لأنه موضع الاجتهاد. كذا في الذخيرة." ولانستطيع أن نقول إنّ ماذهب إليه بعض الصحابة أو التابعين بخلاف ما وقع عليه الإجماع لاحقاً، لم يكن مبنياً على أي دليل، أو لم يكن موضع الاشتباه، فإن القول بلا دليل أو في غير موضع الاشتباه ضلال لا يتصور من خير القرون، والله سبحانه أعلم.
(۱) الفتاوى الهندية ٣:٣٥٧
As for Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), he says: Indeed, later consensus (ijma') removes the earlier disagreement, so the matter does not remain subject to ijtihad after consensus occurs on one of the two schools. For this reason, if a judge ruled on the permissibility of selling umm al-walad, it would not be executed because it contradicts consensus. And since many Hanafi jurists gave fatwa on the execution of the ruling according to the statement of Shuraih regarding accepting women's testimony in hudud and qisas, as will come, after consensus had occurred on not accepting it, the apparent [implication] is that it necessitates that the fatwa be on the statement of the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?) (may Allah have mercy on them). And the statement of the two shaykhs is stronger in evidence, as mentioned by more than one jurist, including Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), that the criterion for a matter being subject to ijtihad is the ambiguity of the evidence, not the reality of the disagreement. It comes in al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya: "In al-Muntaqa, it is indicated that the criterion is the ambiguity of the evidence, not the reality of the disagreement, and thus Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned it in al-Jami' and in al-Siyar al-Kabir. And thus mentioned it the author of al-Aqdiyya. The example mentioned in al-Siyar: If an imam of the Muslims sees fit to accept jizya from the Arab polytheists and accepts it, it is permissible, even if this is considered wrong by everyone, because it is a place of ijtihad. Likewise in al-Dhakhira." And we cannot say that what some Companions or Followers adopted contrary to what later consensus occurred upon was not based on any evidence, or was not a place of ambiguity, for a statement without evidence or without being a place of ambiguity is misguidance, which is inconceivable from the best of generations. And Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage from Imam Muhammad Taqi Usmani's Usul al-Ifta discusses the effect of later ijma on matters previously subject to ijtihad. It begins by stating Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's position: if a later consensus emerges on one of the two opinions in a disputed matter, that matter ceases to be an area of ijtihad. Consequently, a judge's ruling that contradicts this consensus is not enforceable. The author gives two examples: first, the sale of umm al-walad (a slave mother of her master's child) — once consensus formed on its prohibition, a contrary verdict is void. Second, women's testimony in hudud and qisas — though Shuraih permitted it, later consensus rejected it, so some Hanafis who follow Shuraih's position must reconsider. The author then notes that the basis for considering a matter ijtihadi is not mere disagreement but ambiguity in the primary evidence (dalil). He quotes al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya to reinforce this, citing the example from al-Siyar al-Kabir: an imam may accept jizya from Arab polytheists based on his ijtihad, even if the majority disagree, because the evidence is ambiguous. Finally, the author defends earlier Companions and Followers whose views were later contravened by consensus — their positions were based on valid evidence and ambiguity, not error or whim, as that would be unthinkable from the best generation.

Key Terms
الإجماع اللاحق (al-ijma' al-lahiq): A consensus that emerges after an earlier period of disagreement, which according to Imam Muhammad removes the earlier ikhtilaf.
اشتباه الدليل (ishtibah al-dalil): Ambiguity or lack of clarity in the primary textual evidence (Qur'an or Hadith), which allows for legitimate ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary criterion for a legal matter to be considered subject to ijtihad?
A) The number of scholars who hold an opinion
B) The ambiguity of the evidence (ishtibah al-dalil)
C) The existence of disagreement among early jurists
D) The time period in which the issue arose

Key Takeaway
The criterion for ijtihad is ambiguity of evidence, but later consensus on one opinion removes that ambiguity, making the matter non-ijtihadi for fatwa and qada.

Execution of Qada That Is Itself Subject to Ijtihad
Discusses al-Kasani's fourth point and observations on execution of qada that is itself disputed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions among Hanafis on the execution of a qada that is itself subject to ijtihad, and apply the tarjih mechanism to determine the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A qadi's ruling on a disputed matter is not free from ikhtilaf—even among Hanafis, the binding force of such a ruling is contested.

Al-Kasani stated that if the very act of judging a case is a matter of ijtihad (e.g., judgment against an absent person), the ruling is not executed by a judge who does not consider it valid. However, later Hanafi jurists raised two important observations: first, that the example of judgment against an absent person is no longer applicable because it has become effectively agreed upon; second, that the ruling itself is disputed among Hanafis, with some favoring execution. This lesson unpacks the internal Hanafi debate and guides you through the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Al-Kasani's ruling
Identify the fourth point: when the qada itself is subject to ijtihad, it is not executed by those who reject its validity.
Examples: judgment against an absent person, interdiction of a free person.
2
First observation: outdated example
Note that later Hanafis permitted judgment against an absent person due to necessity, so this example no longer represents a disputed matter. Better examples from Ibn 'Abidin: judge ruling for his son, wife, or being flogged for slander.
This shows how rulings evolve with changing circumstances.
3
Second observation: internal ikhtilaf
Contrary to al-Kasani's claim of consensus, Ibn 'Abidin reports a difference: some Hanafis hold the judgment is executed despite the ijtihadi nature. Execution was preferred in al-Khaniyya and al-Zayla'i.
The disagreement is among Hanafis themselves, not just between madhhabs.
4
Tarjih and mufta bihi
Although execution was given preponderance in some later works, the issue remains disputed. A mufti must be aware of this ikhtilaf and the weight of each position.
The mufta bihi may depend on the specific case and the mufti's tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا كان القضاء نفسه مجتهدا فيه والنقطة الرابعة التي ذكرها الكاساني رحمه الله تعالى هى أنه إن كان القضاء نفسه مجتهداً فيه مثل القضاء على الغائب، والحجر على الحرّ، لم ينفُذِ القضاء عند من لا يجوزه. وهناك ملاحظتان على ما ذكره الكاساني رحمه الله تعالى يجب التنبه لهما:
الملاحظة الأولى: أنا قد ذكرنا في مبحث التلفيق عن ابن عابدين رحمه الله تعالى أنّ المتأخرين من الحنفية أجازوا القضاء على الغائب للضرورة والمصلحة. فلا يناسب التمثيل به، لأن القضاء على هذا القول صار متفقاً عليه لمكان الضرورة والمصلحة. ولعل الأمثلة المناسبة لهذا القسم هي التي ذكرها ابن عابدين رحمه الله تعالى، فقال: "كمالو قضى لولده على أجنبي أو لامرأته، أو كان القاضى محدوداً في قذف، لأن نفس القضاء مختلف فيه."
وس كأنه
والملاحظة الثانية: أنّ الكاساني رحمه الله تعالى ذكر حكم هذا القسم متفق عليه فيما بين الحنفية، وأنّهم اتفقوا على أنه إن كان القضاء نفسه مجتهداً فيه، لم ينفذ هذا القضاء عند من لا يعتبره قضاء، فيجوز للقاضي الثاني إن كان ممن لا يعتبره قضاء أن ينقضه. ولكن يظهر من مراجعة الكتب الأخرى فى مذهب الحنفية أنّ هذا الحكم ليس متفقاً عليه فيما بين الحنفية أنفسهم. ولهذا قال ابن عابدين في بيان هذا القسم: "وقسم اختلفوا فيه، وهو الحكم المجتهد فيه، وهو ما يقع الخلاف فيه بعد وجود الحكم، فقيل: ينفذ." فتبين أن حكم هذا القسم مختلف فيه فيما بين الحنفية أنفسهم، وإن وقع تصحيح النّفاذ فى الخانيّة والزَّيْلَعِي وغيرهما، ولكن ذلك لا يُخرجه عن كونه مختلفاً فيه، لكون بعض الحنفية رجحوا النفاذ في هذه الصورة أيضاً،
عدم
If the qada itself is subject to ijtihad — and the fourth point that al-Kasani (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned is that if the qada itself is subject to ijtihad, such as judgment against an absent person, and interdiction of a free person, the judgment is not executed for the one who does not permit it. There are two observations on what al-Kasani mentioned that must be taken note of:
The first observation: We have mentioned in the discussion on talfiq from Ibn 'Abidin (may Allah have mercy on him) that the later Hanafis permitted judgment against an absent person due to necessity and public interest. So it is not appropriate to use it as an example, because judgment according to this view became agreed upon due to necessity and public interest. Perhaps the suitable examples for this section are those that Ibn 'Abidin mentioned, as he said: "Similar to if he judged for his son against a stranger, or for his wife, or if the judge was flogged for slander, because the judgment itself is disputed."
[Unclear text: 'Wa kanna'?]
And the second observation: Al-Kasani (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned the ruling of this section as agreed upon among the Hanafis, that they agreed that if the judgment itself is subject to ijtihad, this judgment is not executed for those who do not consider it a valid judgment, so it is permissible for the second judge if he is among those who do not consider it a valid judgment to nullify it. But it appears from reviewing other books in the Hanafi madhhab that this ruling is not agreed upon among the Hanafis themselves. For this reason, Ibn 'Abidin said in explaining this section: "And a section they differed about, and it is the ruling that is subject to ijtihad, and it is that which disagreement occurs after the existence of the ruling, so it is said: it is executed." So it becomes clear that the ruling of this section is disputed among the Hanafis themselves, even though execution was corrected in al-Khaniyya and al-Zayla'i and others, but that does not remove it from being disputed, because some Hanafis gave preponderance to execution in this case as well.

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth point from al-Kasani in his work concerning the enforcement of qada (judicial decisions). Al-Kasani ruled that if the very act of judging in a particular case is itself a matter of ijtihad (e.g., judgment against an absent person, or placing interdiction on a free person), then such a judgment is not enforceable by a judge who considers that type of judgment invalid. The author (Muhammad Taqi Usmani) then provides two critical observations. First, the example of judgment against an absent person is no longer appropriate because later Hanafi jurists have permitted it for reasons of necessity and public interest, making it effectively agreed upon. Better examples from Ibn 'Abidin include a judge ruling in favor of his own son or wife, or a judge who has been punished for slander hearing a case. Second, contrary to al-Kasani's claim of consensus, the author argues that the ruling on this point is itself disputed among Hanafis. He quotes Ibn 'Abidin who says one opinion holds the judgment is executed despite the ijtihadi nature. Although some later works like al-Khaniyya and al-Zayla'i favored execution, the disagreement remains.

Key Terms
القضاء على الغائب (al-qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): A judgment issued against a person who is not present in court, which is considered a disputed form of qada.
الحجر على الحرّ (al-hajr 'ala al-hurr): Interdiction or restriction placed on a free person, preventing him from disposing of his property, which is a disputed measure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is unanimously accepted.
B) It is unanimously rejected.
C) It is disputed among Hanafis.
D) It is only applicable to non-Hanafi judges.

Key Takeaway
A qada that is itself subject to ijtihad is not automatically unenforceable; the Hanafi school has an internal ikhtilaf on execution, with later works favoring execution.
